5204&#160;years ago</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Periods</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="border:4px solid #0177D3; line-height: 1.5; text-align: center;">
<a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a></div></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Type</th><td class="infobox-data">Cultural</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Criteria</th><td class="infobox-data">i, ii, iii, iv</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Designated</th><td class="infobox-data">1999 <small>(23rd <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Committee" title="World Heritage Committee">session</a>)</small></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Part of</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Neolithic_Orkney" title="Heart of Neolithic Orkney">Heart of Neolithic Orkney</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Reference&#160;no.</th><td class="infobox-data"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://whc.unesco.org/en/list/514">514</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Region</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Europe">Europe</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">
</th></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">
</th></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: #eee;">Identifiers</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Historic_Environment_Scotland" title="Historic Environment Scotland">Historic Environment Scotland</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://portal.historicenvironment.scot/designation/SM90276">SM90276</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Skara Brae</b> <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ær/: &#39;arr&#39; in &#39;marry&#39;">ær</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is a stone-built <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> settlement, located on the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Skaill" title="Bay of Skaill">Bay of Skaill</a> in the parish of <a href="/wiki/Sandwick,_Orkney" title="Sandwick, Orkney">Sandwick</a>, on the west coast of <a href="/wiki/Mainland,_Orkney" title="Mainland, Orkney">Mainland</a>, the largest island in the <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> archipelago of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. It consisted of ten clustered houses, made of <a href="/wiki/Flagstone" title="Flagstone">flagstones</a>, in earthen dams that provided support for the walls; the houses included stone <a href="/wiki/Hearth" title="Hearth">hearths</a>, beds, and cupboards.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A primitive sewer system, with "toilets" and drains in each house,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> included water used to flush waste into a drain and out to the ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>The site was occupied from roughly 3180 BC to about 2500 BC and is Europe's most complete Neolithic village. Skara Brae gained <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> status as one of four sites making up "The <a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Neolithic_Orkney" title="Heart of Neolithic Orkney">Heart of Neolithic Orkney</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Older than <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramids_of_Giza" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Pyramids of Giza">Great Pyramids of Giza</a>, it has been called the "Scottish <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>" because of its excellent preservation.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Care of the site is the responsibility of <a href="/wiki/Historic_Environment_Scotland" title="Historic Environment Scotland">Historic Environment Scotland</a> which works with partners in managing the site: <a href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands_Council" title="Orkney Islands Council">Orkney Islands Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/NatureScot" title="NatureScot">NatureScot</a> (Scottish Natural Heritage), and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Visitors to the site are welcome during much of the year.
</p><p>Uncovered by a storm in 1850, the coastal site may now be at risk from climate change.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Discovery_and_early_exploration">Discovery and early exploration</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery and early exploration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the winter of 1850, a severe storm hit Scotland, causing widespread damage and over 200 deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-bryson2010_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryson2010-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Skaill" title="Bay of Skaill">Bay of Skaill</a>, the storm stripped earth from a large irregular <a href="/wiki/Hillock" title="Hillock">knoll</a>. (The name Skara Brae is a corruption of Skerrabra or Styerrabrae, which originally referred to the knoll.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) When the storm cleared, local villagers found the outline of a village consisting of several small houses without roofs.<sup id="cite_ref-bryson2010_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryson2010-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OSB_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSB-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> William Graham Watt of <a href="/wiki/Skaill_House" title="Skaill House">Skaill House</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a son of the local <a href="/wiki/Laird" title="Laird">laird</a> who was a self-taught <a href="/wiki/Geologist" title="Geologist">geologist</a>, began an amateur excavation of the site, but after four houses were uncovered, work was abandoned in 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-aosb_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aosb-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>The site remained undisturbed until 1913, when during a single weekend, the site was plundered by a party with shovels who took away an unknown quantity of artefacts.<sup id="cite_ref-bryson2010_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryson2010-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1924, another storm swept away part of one of the houses, and it was determined the site should be secured and properly investigated.<sup id="cite_ref-bryson2010_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryson2010-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The job was given to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>'s Professor <a href="/wiki/V._Gordon_Childe" title="V. Gordon Childe">V. Gordon Childe</a>, who travelled to Skara Brae for the first time in mid-1927.<sup id="cite_ref-bryson2010_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryson2010-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019 a series of photographs showing Childe and four women at the site of the excavation were re-examined. It had been widely believed that the women were tourists or local women visiting the excavation site, but a note on the back of  of the photographs identified the women as "4 of [Childe's] lady students", and it seems that they were active participants in the excavation.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The women have been tentatively identified as <a href="/wiki/Margaret_E._B._Simpson" title="Margaret E. B. Simpson">Margaret E. B. Simpson</a> (who was acknowledged in Childe's monographs about Skara Brae), Margaret Mitchell, Mary Kennedy and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Cole" title="Margaret Cole">Margaret Cole</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Neolithic_lifestyle">Neolithic lifestyle</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Neolithic lifestyle"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The inhabitants of Skara Brae were makers and users of <a href="/wiki/Grooved_ware" title="Grooved ware">grooved ware</a>, a distinctive style of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> that had recently appeared in northern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The houses used <a href="/wiki/Earth_sheltering" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth sheltering">earth sheltering</a>: built sunk in the ground, into mounds of prehistoric domestic waste known as <a href="/wiki/Midden" title="Midden">middens</a>.  This provided the houses with stability and also acted as insulation against Orkney's harsh winter climate. On average, each house measures 40 square metres (430&#160;sq&#160;ft) with a large square room containing a stone <a href="/wiki/Hearth" title="Hearth">hearth</a> used for heating and cooking. Given the number of homes, it seems likely that no more than fifty people lived in Skara Brae at any given time.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>It is not clear what material the inhabitants burned in their <a href="/wiki/Hearth" title="Hearth">hearths</a>. Childe was sure that the fuel was <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Childe_1931_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Childe_1931-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but a detailed analysis of vegetation patterns and trends suggests climatic conditions conducive to the development of thick beds of peat did not develop in this part of Orkney until after Skara Brae was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other possible fuels include <a href="/wiki/Driftwood" title="Driftwood">driftwood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dry_dung_fuel" title="Dry dung fuel">animal dung</a>.  There is evidence that dried <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a> may have been used significantly. At some sites in Orkney, investigators have found a glassy, slag-like material called "<a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>" or "cramp" which may be residual burnt seaweed.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The dwellings contain several stone-built pieces of furniture, including <a href="/wiki/Cupboard" title="Cupboard">cupboards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_dresser" title="Welsh dresser">dressers</a>, seats, and storage boxes. Each dwelling was entered through a low doorway with a stone slab door which could be shut "by a bar made of bone that slid in bar-holes cut in the stone door jambs."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several dwellings offered a small connected antechamber, offering access to a partially covered stone drain leading away from the village. It is suggested that these chambers served as indoor toilets.<sup id="cite_ref-Pre_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pre-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Seven of the houses have similar furniture, with the beds and dressers in the same places in each house. The dresser stands against the wall opposite the door and is the first thing seen by anyone entering the dwelling. Each of these houses had a larger bed on the right side of the doorway and a smaller one on the left. <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Laing_(archaeologist)" title="Lloyd Laing (archaeologist)">Lloyd Laing</a> noted that this pattern accorded with <a href="/wiki/Hebridean" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebridean">Hebridean</a> custom up to the early 20th century suggesting that the husband's bed was the larger and the wife's was the smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The discovery of beads and paint pots in some of the smaller beds may support this interpretation. Additional support may come from the recognition that stone boxes lie to the left of most doorways, forcing the person entering the house to turn to the right-hand, "male", side of the dwelling.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At the front of each bed lie the stumps of stone pillars that may have supported a canopy of fur; another link with recent Hebridean style.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Skara_Brae_house_1_5.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Skara_Brae_house_1_5.jpg/220px-Skara_Brae_house_1_5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Skara_Brae_house_1_5.jpg/330px-Skara_Brae_house_1_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Skara_Brae_house_1_5.jpg/440px-Skara_Brae_house_1_5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>Evidence of home furnishings</figcaption></figure>
<p>House 8 has no storage boxes or dresser and has been divided into something resembling small cubicles. Fragments of stone, bone, and antler were excavated suggesting House 8 may have been used to make tools such as bone needles or <a href="/wiki/Flint_axe" title="Flint axe">flint axes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The presence of heat-damaged volcanic rocks and what appears to be a <a href="/wiki/Flue" title="Flue">flue</a>, supports this interpretation. House 8 is distinctive in other ways as well: it is a stand-alone structure not surrounded by midden;<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> instead it is above ground with walls over 2 metres (6.6&#160;ft) thick and has a "porch" protecting the entrance.
</p><p>The site provided the earliest known record of the <a href="/wiki/Human_flea" title="Human flea">human flea (<i>Pulex irritans</i>)</a> in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Grooved_ware" title="Grooved ware">Grooved Ware People</a> who built Skara Brae were primarily <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> who raised cattle, pig and sheep.<sup id="cite_ref-Childe_1931_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Childe_1931-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Childe originally believed that the inhabitants did not farm, but excavations in 1972 unearthed seed grains from a midden suggesting that <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> was cultivated.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fish bones and shells are common in the midden indicating that dwellers ate seafood. Limpet shells are standard and may have been fish bait that was kept in stone boxes in the homes.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The boxes were formed from thin slabs with joints carefully sealed with clay to render them waterproof.
</p><p>This pastoral lifestyle is in sharp contrast to some of the more exotic interpretations of the culture of the Skara Brae people. Euan MacKie suggested that Skara Brae might be the home of a privileged theocratic class of wise men who engaged in astronomical and magical ceremonies at nearby <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Brodgar" title="Ring of Brodgar">Ring of Brodgar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Standing_Stones_of_Stenness" title="Standing Stones of Stenness">Standing Stones of Stenness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Graham and Anna Ritchie cast doubt on this interpretation noting there is no archaeological evidence for this claim,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although a Neolithic "low road" that goes from Skara Brae passes near both these sites and ends at the chambered tomb of <a href="/wiki/Maeshowe" title="Maeshowe">Maeshowe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Low roads connect Neolithic ceremonial sites throughout Britain.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Skara_Brae_house_7.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Skara_Brae_house_7.jpg/220px-Skara_Brae_house_7.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Skara_Brae_house_7.jpg/330px-Skara_Brae_house_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Skara_Brae_house_7.jpg/440px-Skara_Brae_house_7.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>View over the settlement, showing covering to house No. 7 and proximity to modern shore line. The glass roof has now been replaced by a turf one, as the humidity and heat caused by the glass roof were hindering preservation.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Dating_and_abandonment">Dating and abandonment</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Dating and abandonment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Originally, Childe believed that the settlement dated from around 500 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Childe_1931_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Childe_1931-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This interpretation was coming under increasing challenge by the time new excavations in 1972–73 settled the question. <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">Radiocarbon</a> results obtained from samples collected during these excavations indicate that occupation of Skara Brae began about 3180 BC<sup id="cite_ref-Childe_1983_6_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Childe_1983_6-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with occupation continuing for about six hundred years.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Around 2500 BC, after the climate changed, becoming much colder and wetter, the settlement may have been abandoned by its inhabitants. There are many theories as to why the people of Skara Brae left; particularly popular interpretations involving a major storm. Evan Hadingham combined evidence from found objects with the storm scenario to imagine a dramatic end to the settlement:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As was the case at <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, the inhabitants seem to have been taken by surprise and fled in haste, for many of their prized possessions, such as necklaces made from animal teeth and bone, or pins of <a href="/wiki/Walrus_ivory" title="Walrus ivory">walrus ivory</a>, were left behind. The remains of choice meat joints were discovered in some of the beds, presumably forming part of the villagers' last supper. One woman was in such haste that her necklace broke as she squeezed through the narrow doorway of her home, scattering a stream of beads along the passageway outside as she fled the encroaching sand.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Anna Ritchie strongly disagrees with catastrophic interpretations of the village's abandonment:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A popular myth would have the village abandoned during a massive storm that threatened to bury it in sand instantly, but the truth is that its burial was gradual and that it had already been abandoned – for what reason, no one can tell.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The site was farther from the sea than it is today, and it is possible that Skara Brae was built adjacent to a <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> lagoon protected by <a href="/wiki/Dune" title="Dune">dunes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Childe_1983_6_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Childe_1983_6-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although the visible buildings give an impression of an organic whole, certainly, an unknown quantity of additional structures had already been lost to sea erosion before the site's rediscovery and subsequent protection by a <a href="/wiki/Seawall" title="Seawall">seawall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Uncovered remains are known to exist immediately adjacent to the ancient monument in areas presently covered by fields, and others, of uncertain date, can be seen eroding out of the cliff edge a little to the south of the enclosed area.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Artefacts">Artefacts</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Artefacts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Skara_Brae_symbols1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Skara_Brae_symbols1.jpg/220px-Skara_Brae_symbols1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="214" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/Skara_Brae_symbols1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="306" data-file-height="297" /></a><figcaption>Symbols found at Skara Brae and other Neolithic sites</figcaption></figure>
<p>A number of enigmatic <a href="/wiki/Carved_stone_balls" title="Carved stone balls">carved stone balls</a> have been found at the site and some are on display in the museum.<sup id="cite_ref-Carved_Balls_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carved_Balls-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similar objects have been found throughout northern Scotland. The spiral ornamentation on some of these "balls" has been stylistically linked to objects found in the <a href="/wiki/River_Boyne" title="River Boyne">Boyne Valley</a> in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similar symbols have been found carved into stone lintels and bed posts.<sup id="cite_ref-Childe_1931_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Childe_1931-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These symbols, sometimes referred to as "runic writings", have been subjected to controversial translations. For example, author Rodney Castleden suggested that "colons" found punctuating vertical and diagonal symbols may represent separations between words.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Lumps of red <a href="/wiki/Ochre" title="Ochre">ochre</a> found here and at other <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> sites have been interpreted as evidence that <a href="/wiki/Body_painting" title="Body painting">body painting</a> may have been practised.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Nodules of <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">haematite</a> with polished surfaces have been found as well; the shiny surfaces suggest that the nodules were used to finish leather.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other artefacts excavated on site made of animal, fish, bird, and <a href="/wiki/Whalebone" class="mw-redirect" title="Whalebone">whalebone</a>, whale and <a href="/wiki/Walrus_ivory" title="Walrus ivory">walrus ivory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a> teeth included <a href="/wiki/Bradawl" title="Bradawl">awls</a>, needles, knives, <a href="/wiki/Bead" title="Bead">beads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adze" title="Adze">adzes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shovel" title="Shovel">shovels</a>, small bowls and, remarkably, ivory pins up to 25 centimetres (9.8&#160;in) long.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These pins are similar to examples found in <a href="/wiki/Passage_grave" title="Passage grave">passage graves</a> in the Boyne Valley, another piece of evidence suggesting a linkage between the two cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The eponymous <i>Skaill knife</i> was a commonly used tool in Skara Brae; it consists of a large stone flake, with a sharp edge used for cutting, knocked off a sandstone cobble.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This neolithic tool is named after Skara Brae's location in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Skaill" title="Bay of Skaill">Bay of Skaill</a> on <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Skaill knives have been found throughout Orkney and <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>.
</p><p>The 1972 excavations reached layers that had remained waterlogged and had preserved items that otherwise would have been destroyed. These include a twisted skein of heather, one of a very few known examples of Neolithic rope,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a wooden handle.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Related_sites_in_Orkney">Related sites in Orkney</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Related sites in Orkney"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Skarabrae5.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Skara Brae in the sunshine" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Skarabrae5.jpg/220px-Skarabrae5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Skarabrae5.jpg/330px-Skarabrae5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Skarabrae5.jpg/440px-Skarabrae5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a><figcaption>Skara Brae in the sunshine</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Knap_of_Howar" title="Knap of Howar">Knap of Howar</a>, on the Orkney island of <a href="/wiki/Papa_Westray" title="Papa Westray">Papa Westray</a>, is a well-preserved Neolithic farmstead. Dating from 3500&#160;BC to 3100&#160;BC, it is similar in design to Skara Brae, but from an earlier period, and it is thought to be the oldest preserved standing building in northern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Knap_of_Howar_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knap_of_Howar-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Barnhouse_Settlement" title="Barnhouse Settlement">Barnhouse Settlement</a> is by <a href="/wiki/Loch_of_Harray" title="Loch of Harray">Loch of Harray</a> on the Orkney Mainland, not far from the <a href="/wiki/Standing_Stones_of_Stenness" title="Standing Stones of Stenness">Standing Stones of Stenness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Excavations were conducted between 1986 and 1991, over time revealing the base courses of at least 15 houses. The houses have similarities to those of the early phase of Skara Brae in that they have central hearths, beds built against the walls and stone dressers, and internal drains,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but differ in that the houses seem to have been free-standing. The settlement dates back to circa 3000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Agenda_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agenda-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 52">&#58;&#8202;52&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>A comparable, though smaller, site exists at <a href="/wiki/Rinyo" title="Rinyo">Rinyo</a> on <a href="/wiki/Rousay" title="Rousay">Rousay</a>, Orkney. Unusually, no <a href="/wiki/Maeshowe" title="Maeshowe">Maeshowe</a>-type tombs have been found on Rousay and although there are a large number of <a href="/wiki/Chambered_cairn#Orkney-Cromarty" title="Chambered cairn">Orkney–Cromarty chambered cairns</a>, these were built by <a href="/wiki/Unstan_ware" title="Unstan ware">Unstan ware</a> people.
</p><p>There is also a site currently under excavation at <a href="/wiki/Links_of_Noltland" title="Links of Noltland">Links of Noltland</a> on <a href="/wiki/Westray" title="Westray">Westray</a> that appears to have similarities to Skara Brae.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="World_Heritage_status">World Heritage status</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: World Heritage status"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Skara_Brae_ground_plan_1950.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Skara_Brae_ground_plan_1950.jpg/220px-Skara_Brae_ground_plan_1950.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Skara_Brae_ground_plan_1950.jpg/330px-Skara_Brae_ground_plan_1950.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Skara_Brae_ground_plan_1950.jpg/440px-Skara_Brae_ground_plan_1950.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4845" data-file-height="3401" /></a><figcaption>Site Plan</figcaption></figure>
<p>"The <a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Neolithic_Orkney" title="Heart of Neolithic Orkney">Heart of Neolithic Orkney</a>" was inscribed as a World Heritage site in December 1999. In addition to Skara Brae the site includes <a href="/wiki/Maeshowe" title="Maeshowe">Maeshowe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Brodgar" title="Ring of Brodgar">Ring of Brodgar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Standing_Stones_of_Stenness" title="Standing Stones of Stenness">Standing Stones of Stenness</a> and other nearby sites. It is managed by <a href="/wiki/Historic_Environment_Scotland" title="Historic Environment Scotland">Historic Environment Scotland</a>, whose "Statement of Significance" for the site begins:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The monuments at the heart of Neolithic Orkney and Skara Brae proclaim the triumphs of the human spirit in early ages and isolated places. They were approximately contemporary with the <a href="/wiki/Mastaba" title="Mastaba">mastabas</a> of the archaic period of Egypt (first and second dynasties), the brick temples of Sumeria, and the first cities of the Harappa culture in India, and a century or two earlier than the Golden Age of China. Unusually fine for their early date, and with a remarkably rich survival of evidence, these sites stand as a visible symbol of the achievements of early peoples away from the traditional centres of civilisation.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Some areas and facilities were closed due to the worldwide <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> during parts of 2020 and into 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Risk_from_climate_change">Risk from climate change</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Risk from climate change"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2019, a risk assessment was performed to assess the site's <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_vulnerability" title="Climate change vulnerability">vulnerability to climate change</a>. The report by <a href="/wiki/Historic_Environment_Scotland" title="Historic Environment Scotland">Historic Environment Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands_Council" title="Orkney Islands Council">Orkney Islands Council</a> and others concludes that the entire Heart of Neolithic Orkney World Heritage Site, and in particular Skara Brae, is "extremely vulnerable" to climate change due to rising sea levels, increased rainfall and other factors; it also highlights the risk that Skara Brae could be partially destroyed by one unusually severe storm.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li>The 1968 children's novel <i>The Boy with the Bronze Axe</i> by <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Fidler" title="Kathleen Fidler">Kathleen Fidler</a> is set during the last days of Skara Brae.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramwell2009182–185_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramwell2009182–185-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This theme is also adopted by <a href="/wiki/Rosemary_Sutcliff" title="Rosemary Sutcliff">Rosemary Sutcliff</a> in her 1977 novel <i>Shifting Sands</i>, in which the evacuation of the site is portrayed as unhurried, with most of the inhabitants surviving.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramwell2009185–186_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramwell2009185–186-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Ireland" title="Music of Ireland">Irish traditional music</a> group <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae_(band)" title="Skara Brae (band)">Skara Brae</a> took their name from the settlement. Their only album <i><a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae_(album)" title="Skara Brae (album)">Skara Brae</a></i> was released in 1971 and reissued on CD in 1998.</li>
<li>A stone was unveiled in Skara Brae on 12 April 2008 marking the anniversary of Soviet cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> becoming the first man to orbit the Earth in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-Gagarin1_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gagarin1-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gagarin2_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gagarin2-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The video game <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bard%27s_Tale" title="The Bard&#39;s Tale">The Bard's Tale</a></i> takes place in a fictionalised version of Skara Brae.</li>
<li>The video game <i><a href="/wiki/Starsiege:_Tribes" title="Starsiege: Tribes">Starsiege: Tribes</a></i> features an iconic map named "Scarabrae".</li>
<li>The video game series <i><a href="/wiki/Ultima_(series)" title="Ultima (series)">Ultima</a></i> includes the city of Skara Brae, which is on an island to the west of the main continent. It is devoted to the virtue of Spirituality, located next to a moongate and is the home of Shamino the Ranger.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Kim_Stanley_Robinson" title="Kim Stanley Robinson">Kim Stanley Robinson</a>'s 1991 novelette <i>A History of the Twentieth Century, with Illustrations</i>, the main character visits Skara Brae and other Orkney Island neolithic sites as part of a journey he takes to gain perspective on the violent history of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones_and_the_Kingdom_of_the_Crystal_Skull" title="Indiana Jones and the Kingdom of the Crystal Skull">Indiana Jones and the Kingdom of the Crystal Skull</a></i>, Jones is shown lecturing to his students about the site,<sup id="cite_ref-SB_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SB-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 6">&#58;&#8202;6&#8202;</span></sup> where he gives the date as "3100 BC".</li>
<li>Skara Brae is used as the name for a New York Scottish pub in the <a href="/wiki/IDW_Publishing" title="IDW Publishing">IDW</a> <a href="/wiki/Teenage_Mutant_Ninja_Turtles_(IDW_Publishing)" title="Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles (IDW Publishing)"><i>Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles</i> comic series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Skara Brae's ancient sewer system and its use of running water is referenced by Medical Examiner <a href="/wiki/List_of_NCIS_characters#Dr._Donald_&quot;Ducky&quot;_Mallard" title="List of NCIS characters">Dr. Donald "Ducky" Mallard</a> in <a href="/wiki/NCIS_(TV_series)" title="NCIS (TV series)"><i>NCIS</i></a> episode <a href="/wiki/NCIS_season_6" title="NCIS season 6">"Murder 2.0" (season 6, episode 6)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skara_Brae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is one of four <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a> in Scotland, the others being the <a href="/wiki/Old_Town,_Edinburgh" title="Old Town, Edinburgh">Old Town</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Town,_Edinburgh" title="New Town, Edinburgh">New Town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>; <a href="/wiki/New_Lanark" title="New Lanark">New Lanark</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Lanarkshire" title="South Lanarkshire">South Lanarkshire</a>; and <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Scotland" title="St Kilda, Scotland">St Kilda</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Western Isles</a></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
